This is explicitly mentioned in the FSM as a symptom of a defective cylinder head gasket. This internal pressure buildup likely caused the radiator hose to burst and led to the immediate overheating. The next step is to perform a cylinder leak-down test to confirm the internal leak and identify the affected cylinder(s).\",\n  \"mechanic_instructions\": \"The air bubbles in the cooling water confirm that combustion gases are entering the cooling system, most likely due to a defective cylinder head gasket or a crack in the cylinder head or block. This internal pressure is what caused the hose to burst and the engine to overheat. To pinpoint the exact source of the leak, you need to perform a cylinder leak-down test. Also check the kickdown switch at the throttle pedal (under carpet). Clean or replace the switch if corroded. Adjust cable per factory specification. NOTE: Proper kickdown cable adjustment also affects all shift points — not just wide-open-throttle downshifts. Improper adjustment causes generally poor shift behavior.",

The FSM outlines specific checks for the accelerator linkage and kickdown function. \\n\\n1.  **Accelerator Linkage Adjustment:** The accelerator pedal must physically reach the kickdown stop, and the regulating linkage must ensure the throttle valve lever reaches its full throttle stop. If this mechanical travel is not achieved, the kickdown signal will not be sent, and the engine will not receive full throttle. (Source 9, 7)\\n2.  **Kickdown Switch Function:** The kickdown switch, located below the accelerator pedal, is an electrical component that signals the transmission for a downshift. If the switch itself is faulty or its wiring is compromised, the electrical signal will not reach the transmission. (Source 14, 15, 35)\\n3.  **Transmission Vacuum Control:** The automatic transmission uses a vacuum box connected via a vacuum line to modulate shift points based on engine load. An incorrect or absent vacuum signal can prevent proper transmission response, including kickdown. (Source 19, 56, 28)\\n\\nI will start by having you verify the physical movement and activation of the linkage and switch, as these are the most common and easiest to check initial points of failure for this symptom.\",\n  \"mechanic_instructions\": \"The transmission's inability to downshift on kickdown indicates a failure in the system that signals the transmission for maximum acceleration. The FSM outlines specific checks for the accelerator linkage and kickdown function. \n\n1.  **Accelerator Linkage Adjustment:** The accelerator pedal must physically reach the kickdown stop, and the regulating linkage must ensure the throttle valve lever reaches its full throttle stop. If this mechanical travel is not achieved, the kickdown signal will not be sent, and the engine will not receive full throttle. (Source 9, 7)\n2.  **Kickdown Switch Function:** The kickdown switch, located below the accelerator pedal, is an electrical component that signals the transmission for a downshift. If the switch itself is faulty or its wiring is compromised, the electrical signal will not reach the transmission. (Source 14, 15, 35)\n3.  **Transmission Vacuum Control:** The automatic transmission uses a vacuum box connected via a vacuum line to modulate shift points based on engine load. An incorrect or absent vacuum signal can prevent proper transmission response, including kickdown. (Source 19, 56, 28)\n\nI will start by having you verify the physical movement and activation of the linkage and switch, as these are the most common and easiest to check initial points of failure for this symptom.",

NOTE: Proper kickdown cable adjustment also affects all shift points — not just wide-open-throttle downshifts. Improper adjustment causes generally poor shift behavior.",

When the accelerator pedal is fully depressed to the kickdown stop, the throttle valve lever MUST rest against the full throttle stop of the valve connection. Since it is not, the linkage needs adjustment. Locate the hex bolt (indicated by an arrow in FSM figures) on the regulating shaft above the accelerator pedal. Loosen this bolt, push the regulating linkage up to the full throttle stop on the valve connection, and then tighten the hex bolt. This ensures the full range of motion for the throttle and proper kickdown switch engagement. Refer to FSM Job No. 07.4.1-440 or 07.4.1-443 for detailed adjustment procedures, specifically Figure 3 in 07.4.1-440 or Figure 5 in 07.4.1-443.\",\n  \"answer_path_prompts\": [\n    \"After adjustment, the throttle valve lever now rests against the full throttle stop when the pedal is at kickdown, and the transmission downshifts correctly.\",\n    \"After adjustment, the throttle valve lever now rests against the full throttle stop when the pedal is at kickdown, but the transmission still does not downshift.\",\n    \"I cannot achieve proper adjustment; the linkage seems bent or binding, preventing the throttle from reaching its full stop.\"\n  ],\n  \"requires_input\": true\n}",

This prevents the engine from reaching full throttle and the kickdown switch from being properly actuated, thus inhibiting the transmission's kickdown function.       "mechanic_instructions": "The problem is in the accelerator regulating linkage adjustment. When the accelerator pedal is fully depressed to the kickdown stop, the throttle valve lever MUST rest against the full throttle stop of the valve connection. Since it is not, the linkage needs adjustment. Locate the hex bolt (indicated by an arrow in FSM figures) on the regulating shaft above the accelerator pedal. Loosen this bolt, push the regulating linkage up to the full throttle stop on the valve connection, and then tighten the hex bolt. This ensures the full range of motion for the throttle and proper kickdown switch engagement. Refer to FSM Job No. 07.4.1-440 or 07.4.1-443 for detailed adjustment procedures, specifically Figure 3 in 07.4.1-440 or Figure 5 in 07.4.1-443.",

When the accelerator pedal is fully depressed to the kickdown stop, the throttle valve lever MUST rest against the full throttle stop of the valve connection. Since it is not, the linkage needs adjustment. Locate the hex bolt (indicated by an arrow in FSM figures) on the regulating shaft above the accelerator pedal. ],\n  \"diagnostic_reasoning\": \"The reported symptom of no downshift on kickdown points to a failure in the kickdown system. This system relies on correct mechanical linkage adjustment to ensure the throttle valve lever reaches its full stop and the kickdown switch is activated. If the linkage is out of adjustment, the full throttle signal or kickdown switch activation may not occur. If the linkage is correct, the kickdown switch itself could be faulty, failing to send the electrical signal to the transmission. Finally, if both mechanical and electrical signals are present, the issue could be within the transmission's control pressure system or kickdown solenoid. The FSM outlines procedures for adjusting the accelerator pedal and linkage to ensure the throttle valve lever reaches the full load stop and the kickdown switch is engaged (Source 1, 3, 4, 7, 10). The kickdown solenoid switch location is also identified (Source 24, 25, 37). Use Lemforder or Meyle replacements (~$400+ for the set). This requires supporting the engine/subframe and partially separating the subframe from the body. NOTE: Labor-intensive but the improvement in handling and steering precision is transformative. Often combined with the subframe recall repair and a complete front-end rebuild.",

These joints are designed to be 'service-free' with a lifetime grease charge, sealed by the rubber sleeves. Damage to these sleeves allows dirt and moisture to enter, leading to rapid wear of the ball joint itself. The FSM explicitly states: 'If rubber sleeve on a used joint shows evidence of damage, renew complete joint.' Therefore, the entire track rod end or drag link must be replaced, not just the sleeve, as the internal components are compromised.\",\n  \"mechanic_instructions\": \"The damaged rubber sleeves on the track rod and/or drag link ball joints are the cause of your front-end clunking, vague steering, and subframe movement. These sleeves are critical for sealing the 'service-free' ball joints. Once compromised, dirt enters, and the joint wears rapidly. As per FSM procedure 46.1-540 and 46.1-550, if a rubber sleeve on a used joint is damaged, the entire track rod end or drag link must be replaced. Proceed with replacing the affected track rod ends and/or drag link. Refer to FSM section 46.1-540 for Track Rod removal and installation (Source 1, page 2) and 46.1-550 for Drag Link removal and installation (Source 29, page 1). Remember to replace all self-locking hex nuts on principle and check toe-in after replacement (Source 2, page 3).\",\n  \"answer_path_prompts\": [],\n  \"requires_input\": false\n}",

      "mechanic_instructions": "The damaged rubber sleeves on the track rod and/or drag link ball joints are the cause of your front-end clunking, vague steering, and subframe movement. These sleeves are critical for sealing the 'service-free' ball joints. Once compromised, dirt enters, and the joint wears rapidly. As per FSM procedure 46.1-540 and 46.1-550, if a rubber sleeve on a used joint is damaged, the entire track rod end or drag link must be replaced. Proceed with replacing the affected track rod ends and/or drag link. Refer to FSM section 46.1-540 for Track Rod removal and installation (Source 1, page 2) and 46.1-550 for Drag Link removal and installation (Source 29, page 1). Remember to replace all self-locking hex nuts on principle and check toe-in after replacement (Source 2, page 3).",

These sleeves are critical for sealing the 'service-free' ball joints. Once compromised, dirt enters, and the joint wears rapidly. Refer to FSM section 46.1-540 for Track Rod removal and installation (Source 1, page 2) and 46.1-550 for Drag Link removal and installation (Source 29, page 1). While the FSM does not detail battery tray replacement or corrosion repair, the first step is to disconnect the negative battery cable to prevent further acid damage and electrical shorts. Remove battery, neutralize acid with baking soda and water, wire brush all corrosion, treat with rust converter, coat with acid-resistant paint (POR-15). If perforated, weld in new metal. Install new hold-down bracket. Coat terminals with anti-corrosion spray. NOTE: The R107 has the battery in the trunk (right side). Check surrounding trunk floor — acid migration corrodes adjacent areas. Keep the battery vent tube connected and routed outside.",

This indicates the primary issue is localized to the tray and not a deeper structural problem. The FSM does not provide specific procedures for battery tray corrosion repair or replacement. The FSM does, however, provide guidance on battery care to prevent future issues.\",\n  \"mechanic_instructions\": \"Given the corrosion is superficial and limited to the battery tray, proceed as follows:\\n\\n1.  **Neutralize and Clean:** Thoroughly clean the corroded area with a baking soda and water solution to neutralize any remaining acid. Rinse with clean water and dry completely. Use a wire brush to remove loose corrosion.\\n2.  **Inspect and Protect:** Inspect the tray for any perforations. If none, apply a rust-inhibiting primer and then a protective coating (e.g., heavy-duty paint or undercoating) to the tray to prevent future corrosion.\\n3.  **Secure the Battery:** Once the tray is dry and protected, reinstall the battery. Ensure the battery hold-down clamp is properly secured and tightened to prevent any movement. An unsecured battery can lead to further acid spills and damage.\\n4.  **Battery Maintenance:** To prevent future corrosion, ensure battery terminals are clean and coated with anti-corrosion grease. Regularly check the battery's acid level and specific gravity, especially in hot climates, as outlined in FSM sections 54.1-020 and 54.0-020 (pages 1, 'Layoff' and 'Self-discharge'). Oxidized terminals should be cleaned. Ensure the battery is recharged monthly if laid off to prevent discharge, which can lead to acid stratification and potential leaks. This concludes the repair for the corroded battery tray.\",\n  \"requires_input\": false\n}",

      "mechanic_instructions": "Given the corrosion is superficial and limited to the battery tray, proceed as follows:\n\n1.  **Neutralize and Clean:** Thoroughly clean the corroded area with a baking soda and water solution to neutralize any remaining acid. Rinse with clean water and dry completely. Use a wire brush to remove loose corrosion.\n2.  **Inspect and Protect:** Inspect the tray for any perforations. If none, apply a rust-inhibiting primer and then a protective coating (e.g., heavy-duty paint or undercoating) to the tray to prevent future corrosion.\n3.  **Secure the Battery:** Once the tray is dry and protected, reinstall the battery. Ensure the battery hold-down clamp is properly secured and tightened to prevent any movement. An unsecured battery can lead to further acid spills and damage.\n4.  **Battery Maintenance:** To prevent future corrosion, ensure battery terminals are clean and coated with anti-corrosion grease. Regularly check the battery's acid level and specific gravity, especially in hot climates, as outlined in FSM sections 54.1-020 and 54.0-020 (pages 1, 'Layoff' and 'Self-discharge'). Oxidized terminals should be cleaned. Ensure the battery is recharged monthly if laid off to prevent discharge, which can lead to acid stratification and potential leaks. This concludes the repair for the corroded battery tray.",

Rinse with clean water and dry completely. Use a wire brush to remove loose corrosion.\n2.  **Inspect and Protect:** Inspect the tray for any perforations. If none, apply a rust-inhibiting primer and then a protective coating (e.g., heavy-duty paint or undercoating) to the tray to prevent future corrosion.\n3.  **Secure the Battery:** Once the tray is dry and protected, reinstall the battery. Ensure the battery hold-down clamp is properly secured and tightened to prevent any movement. An unsecured battery can lead to further acid spills and damage.\n4.  **Battery Maintenance:** To prevent future corrosion, ensure battery terminals are clean and coated with anti-corrosion grease. Regularly check the battery's acid level and specific gravity, especially in hot climates, as outlined in FSM sections 54.1-020 and 54.0-020 (pages 1, 'Layoff' and 'Self-discharge'). Oxidized terminals should be cleaned. Ensure the battery is recharged monthly if laid off to prevent discharge, which can lead to acid stratification and potential leaks. This concludes the repair for the corroded battery tray.",

The 1976 US-spec 450SL has a belt-driven pump that injects fresh air into the exhaust manifolds for catalytic converter efficiency. If pump bearings fail, it produces grinding/squealing noise. Also check the air pump check valves and diverter valve. NOTE: Required for emissions compliance in inspection states. In non-emission states, some owners remove it — verify legality first. A non-functional air pump does not affect driveability.",

Also check the air pump check valves and diverter valve. NOTE: Required for emissions compliance in inspection states. In non-emission states, some owners remove it — verify legality first. A non-functional air pump does not affect driveability.",

The most common and easiest-to-verify cause of squealing is incorrect V-belt tension or a worn belt. Grinding typically points to a failing bearing in a pulley or an accessory. The FSM provides specific procedures for checking and adjusting V-belt tension, making this the logical first diagnostic step. If the belts are correctly tensioned and in good condition, the focus will shift to isolating the specific accessory or idler pulley with a failing bearing.\",\n  \"mechanic_instructions\": \"The noise originating from the accessory drive belt suggests either an issue with the belt itself or a bearing in one of the components it drives. The first step is to visually inspect the V-belts for cracks, glazing, or fraying, and then check their tension. Incorrect tension is a common cause of squealing. Refer to FSM section 13.1-340 for V-belt tensioning procedures, specifically Fig. 3 for general V-belts, Fig. 4 for double V-belt B, and Fig. 6 for V-belt C.\",\n  \"answer_path_prompts\": [\n    \"The V-belt(s) are visibly loose, cracked, or glazed, and adjusting tension or replacing the belt eliminates or significantly reduces the noise.\",\n    \"The V-belt(s) appear to be in good condition and are properly tensioned, but the squealing or grinding noise persists.\",\n    \"The noise changes significantly or disappears when a specific accessory (e.g., A/C compressor, power steering pump, alternator) is engaged or disengaged, or when its belt is temporarily removed (for diagnostic purposes only, do not run engine for extended periods without critical belts).\"\n  ],\n  \"requires_input\": true\n}",

    "mechanic_instructions": "Your clarification indicates the damage is on the main center console, not just the shift lever cover. To properly access and address the worn shift pattern and cracked shift gate plastic, you must remove the entire center console. This is a multi-step process.\n\nRefer to FSM 68.1-200, 'Removal and Installation of Center Console' (Source 38, page 1; Source 46, page 19; Source 7, page 2; Source 36, page 20).\n\n**Center Console Removal Procedure:**\n1.  Remove the steering wheel (FSM 46.1-610 - *procedure not provided in retrieved documents*).\n2.  Remove the driver's and front passenger's seats (FSM 91.1-100 - *procedure not provided in retrieved documents*).\n3.  Remove both front floor mats.\n4.  Remove the cover on the shift lever (FSM 68.1-230 - *see Source 4, page 1*).\n5.  Remove both covers under the instrument panel (FSM 68.1-150 - *procedure not provided in retrieved documents*).\n6.  Remove screws (1) at the rear of the tunnel (Fig. 1, Source 38, page 1).\n7.  Loosen carpet (2) on the tunnel from the center console (Fig. 1, Source 38, page 1).\n8.  Remove screws (3) left and right on the front wall (Fig. 2, Source 38, page 1).\n9.  Pull out the heater control lever.\n10. Force molding (4) on control levers at the upper end out of the housing with a plastic wedge (Fig. 3, Source 38, page 1).\n11. Unscrew the two screws (5) on cover (6) (Fig. 4, Source 7, page 2; Source 36, page 20).\n12. Remove the cover with switches and pull off plugs.\n13. Remove the radio.\n14. Remove screws (7) (Fig. 5, Source 7, page 2; Source 36, page 20).\n15. Disconnect the connecting cable for both lights (8) on cable connector (9) and pull off lighting cable (10) (Fig. 5, Source 7, page 2; Source 36, page 20).\n16. *Only with automatic transmission:* Loosen the gate plate on the floor shift.\n17.     "validated_fix": "Replace the torn CV boot(s) and repack with fresh CV grease, or replace the entire CV joint if it has been running without grease and is now worn (clicking sound = worn). The R107 independent rear suspension uses CV joints (constant velocity joints) at the rear axle half-shafts. A torn boot allows grease to escape and contamination to enter, destroying the joint. If caught early (boot torn but no clicking), replacing the boot and repacking is sufficient. If clicking, the joint must be replaced. NOTE: Also check the inner CV joints/flex discs at the differential output flanges.",

The grease splatter indicates a torn CV boot, allowing the lubricating grease to escape and contaminants to enter the joint, leading to wear and the characteristic clicking noise, especially under load during turns. While the FSM does not explicitly detail 'CV joint failure' as a diagnostic procedure for these symptoms, it does provide procedures for the 'Removal and Installation of Rear Axle Center Housing with Rear Axle Shafts' (Source 6, 8) and 'Complete Remove and Installation of Rear Axle Shaft' (Source 10, 40), confirming the presence and serviceability of these components.\",\n  \"mechanic_instructions\": \"The clicking noise and grease splatter point directly to a compromised rear axle shaft, most likely a CV joint. You need to visually inspect the rear axle shafts and their rubber boots. Jack up the rear of the vehicle, support it securely, and remove the rear wheels. Carefully examine both inner and outer CV boots on each axle shaft for any tears, cracks, or signs of grease leakage.     "validated_fix": "Replace the torn CV boot(s) and repack with fresh CV grease, or replace the entire CV joint if it has been running without grease and is now worn (clicking sound = worn). The R107 independent rear suspension uses CV joints (constant velocity joints) at the rear axle half-shafts. A torn boot allows grease to escape and contamination to enter, destroying the joint. If caught early (boot torn but no clicking), replacing the boot and repacking is sufficient. If clicking, the joint must be replaced. NOTE: Also check the inner CV joints/flex discs at the differential output flanges.",

    "validated_fix": "Replace the torn CV boot(s) and repack with fresh CV grease, or replace the entire CV joint if it has been running without grease and is now worn (clicking sound = worn). The R107 independent rear suspension uses CV joints (constant velocity joints) at the rear axle half-shafts. A torn boot allows grease to escape and contamination to enter, destroying the joint. If caught early (boot torn but no clicking), replacing the boot and repacking is sufficient. If clicking, the joint must be replaced. NOTE: Also check the inner CV joints/flex discs at the differential output flanges.",

While the rubber boots appear intact, the grease splatter indicates a leak. This leak could be a very subtle tear in a boot that was missed, a failure of an internal seal within the CV joint itself, or a leak from a different component, such as a sealing ring on the rear axle housing (e.g., item 22 or 25 in Fig. 1, Source 7), with the leaked fluid being mistaken for CV grease. The FSM details procedures for replacing rubber sleeves (boots) and sealing rings on the rear axle shaft and housing (Source 1, 7, 24, 26, 32, 37, 42, 46, 53, 55). To isolate the cause, we need to determine the nature of the leaked fluid and check for play in the CV joints.\",\n  \"mechanic_instructions\": \"Even with seemingly intact boots, the clicking noise and grease splatter point to a CV joint issue or a related seal failure.     "technical_issue": "CV Joint Boot Tear / CV Joint Failure",
    "validated_fix": "Replace the torn CV boot(s) and repack with fresh CV grease, or replace the entire CV joint if it has been running without grease and is now worn (clicking sound = worn). The R107 independent rear suspension uses CV joints (constant velocity joints) at the rear axle half-shafts. A torn boot allows grease to escape and contamination to enter, destroying the joint. If caught early (boot torn but no clicking), replacing the boot and repacking is sufficient. If clicking, the joint must be replaced. NOTE: Also check the inner CV joints/flex discs at the differential output flanges.",
    "vehicle_id": "1976_mercedes_450sl",

However, a clicking noise from the rear wheels when turning is a classic symptom of internal wear within a Constant Velocity (CV) joint. Even with an intact boot, the internal bearings or cage can wear, leading to excessive play and noise, especially under the varying torque loads experienced during turns. The FSM documents retrieved do not contain specific diagnostic procedures for a clicking noise from the rear axle or for internal CV joint wear, only general removal/installation of rear axle shafts. Therefore, the next steps are based on general mechanical diagnostic principles for CV joints.\",\n  \"mechanic_instructions\": \"Understood, no visible boot damage or grease. The clicking noise when turning still strongly indicates internal wear in a rear CV joint. The joint's internal components can fail even if the boot remains intact. We need to isolate which side the noise is coming from and confirm it's the CV joint. Perform the following test to reproduce and localize the noise:\\n\\n1.  With the vehicle safely on the ground, drive slowly in tight circles, first to the left, then to the right. Listen carefully to determine if the clicking is louder or only present when turning in one specific direction (e.g., louder when turning left, indicating a problem with the right CV joint, or vice-versa).\\n2.  If possible, with the vehicle safely lifted on a hoist, have a helper slowly rotate the rear wheels by hand while you listen closely to the CV joints for any binding or clicking. Check for any noticeable play in the axle shafts by attempting to twist them by hand while observing the CV joints.\",\n  \"answer_path_prompts\": [\n    \"The clicking noise is distinctly louder or only occurs when turning left.\",\n    \"The clicking noise is distinctly louder or only occurs when turning right.\",\n    \"The clicking noise is present when turning in both directions, or I can feel excessive play in one or both axle shafts when twisting them by hand.\",\n    \"I cannot reproduce the clicking noise during the test, or the noise sounds different than a typical CV joint click (e.g., more of a clunk, grind, or hum).\"\n  ],\n  \"confidence_disclaimer\": \"The retrieved FSM documents do not contain specific diagnostic procedures for a 'clicking noise from the rear wheels when turning' or for internal wear of rear CV joints.       "mechanic_instructions": "Understood, no visible boot damage or grease. The clicking noise when turning still strongly indicates internal wear in a rear CV joint. The joint's internal components can fail even if the boot remains intact. We need to isolate which side the noise is coming from and confirm it's the CV joint. Perform the following test to reproduce and localize the noise:\n\n1.  With the vehicle safely on the ground, drive slowly in tight circles, first to the left, then to the right. Listen carefully to determine if the clicking is louder or only present when turning in one specific direction (e.g., louder when turning left, indicating a problem with the right CV joint, or vice-versa).\n2.  If possible, with the vehicle safely lifted on a hoist, have a helper slowly rotate the rear wheels by hand while you listen closely to the CV joints for any binding or clicking. Remove the windshield (requires piano wire or specialized cutting tool and a helper), clean all old adhesive from the body pinchweld and the glass edge, treat any rust with converter and primer, and reinstall with new urethane adhesive (3M or Sika). Replace the chrome or rubber windshield moldings. NOTE: The windshield bond is structural on the R107 — it provides body rigidity critical for an open-top car. Professional installation is strongly recommended. Also inspect the cowl/scuttle area for rust while the windshield is out — this is an excellent time to address hidden corrosion.",

The windshield post weatherstrip (FSM 68.1-550) and the ornamental frame (FSM 68.1-540) are the primary components in this region responsible for sealing. The next step is to pinpoint the exact point of ingress to determine if the weatherstrip itself, its attachment, or the interface with the windshield or body is at fault.\",\n  \"mechanic_instructions\": \"Your observation that water appears when sprayed on the A-pillar trim and weatherstrip confirms the leak is in this area. The next step is to pinpoint the exact entry point. The windshield post weatherstrip (FSM 68.1-550) and the ornamental frame (FSM 68.1-540) are secured by screws and clips, and rely on proper seating and rubber integrity for a watertight seal. Visually inspect the entire length of the A-pillar weatherstrip and the ornamental frame for any visible cracks, tears, shrinkage, or signs of improper seating against the body or windshield. Pay close attention to the areas around the attachment screws and clips. Then, perform a more precise water test. Use a spray bottle or a very low-pressure hose stream to apply water directly to specific, small sections of the A-pillar weatherstrip and the seam where it meets the windshield and the body. Have your helper inside the car observe closely for the exact point of ingress.\",\n  \"answer_path_prompts\": [\n    \"Water appears immediately when sprayed directly onto the rubber weatherstrip itself, indicating a crack or tear in the rubber.\",\n    \"Water appears only when sprayed around the screws or clips that secure the weatherstrip to the A-pillar.\",\n    \"Water appears when sprayed along the seam where the weatherstrip meets the windshield glass or the body, but not directly on the weatherstrip itself.\",\n    \"The leak is not visible with targeted spraying on the A-pillar weatherstrip or ornamental frame, suggesting it might be originating from the main windshield seal itself, or tracking from a higher point.\"\n  ],\n  \"requires_input\": true\n}",

    "mechanic_instructions": "Your observation that water appears when sprayed on the A-pillar trim and weatherstrip confirms the leak is in this area. The next step is to pinpoint the exact entry point. The windshield post weatherstrip (FSM 68.1-550) and the ornamental frame (FSM 68.1-540) are secured by screws and clips, and rely on proper seating and rubber integrity for a watertight seal. Visually inspect the entire length of the A-pillar weatherstrip and the ornamental frame for any visible cracks, tears, shrinkage, or signs of improper seating against the body or windshield. Pay close attention to the areas around the attachment screws and clips. Then, perform a more precise water test. Use a spray bottle or a very low-pressure hose stream to apply water directly to specific, small sections of the A-pillar weatherstrip and the seam where it meets the windshield and the body. Have your helper inside the car observe closely for the exact point of ingress.",

    "validated_fix": "Replace the brake vacuum booster. Test: with the engine off, pump the brake pedal several times to deplete vacuum, then hold the pedal and start the engine — the pedal should drop noticeably as vacuum assist engages. If it doesn't, the booster has failed. Also check the vacuum check valve and the vacuum supply hose from the intake manifold. Replace the booster with a rebuilt or new unit (ATE or FTE). Bench-bleed and install the master cylinder onto the new booster. Bleed the entire brake system. NOTE: The booster is behind the master cylinder on the firewall. Replacement requires disconnecting brake lines and the master cylinder — bleed everything after reassembly.",

    "validated_fix": "Replace the brake vacuum booster. Test: with the engine off, pump the brake pedal several times to deplete vacuum, then hold the pedal and start the engine — the pedal should drop noticeably as vacuum assist engages. If it doesn't, the booster has failed. Also check the vacuum check valve and the vacuum supply hose from the intake manifold. Replace the booster with a rebuilt or new unit (ATE or FTE). Bench-bleed and install the master cylinder onto the new booster. Bleed the entire brake system. NOTE: The booster is behind the master cylinder on the firewall. Replacement requires disconnecting brake lines and the master cylinder — bleed everything after reassembly.",

This system relies on engine vacuum to operate the brake unit (booster). The most probable causes are a faulty brake unit itself, a leak in the vacuum line to the brake unit, or a defective check valve that maintains vacuum in the booster when the engine is off. The initial test is designed to quickly differentiate between a functional booster with good vacuum retention and a system with a fault in either the booster or its vacuum supply.\",\n  \"mechanic_instructions\": \"To diagnose the hard brake pedal, we need to check the brake unit (booster) and its vacuum supply.     "response_raw": "{\n  \"current_state\": \"PHASE_B_FUNNEL\",\n  \"confidence\": \"general_knowledge\",\n  \"intersecting_subsystems\": [\n    \"Brake System\",\n    \"Vacuum System\"\n  ],\n  \"source_citations\": [\n    {\n      \"source\": \"80.0-030 Design and Operation of Backrest Interlock.pdf\",\n      \"page\": 1,\n      \"context\": \"Design and Operation of Backrest Interlock\"\n    },\n    {\n      \"source\": \"80_Vacuum_System_volume_1.pdf\",\n      \"page\": 11,\n      \"context\": \"Design and Operation of Backrest Interlock\"\n    }\n  ],\n  \"diagnostic_reasoning\": \"A distinct hissing sound with the engine running, coupled with a hard brake pedal, is a classic indication of a vacuum leak. The brake booster relies on engine vacuum to provide power assist. If there is a leak in the booster, its check valve, or the vacuum supply hose, the vacuum differential required for assist will be compromised, resulting in a hard pedal. The FSM confirms that engine vacuum is used for various systems, including the backrest interlock, indicating the presence of a vacuum system connected to the intake manifold.     "mechanic_instructions": "The rusted-through jack point is compromised. Do not attempt to use it. You must use alternative, structurally sound jacking points. \n\nFor lifting the front of the vehicle with a shop jack or pit lift, position the lift against the **front axle carrier** (refer to Fig. 2 in FSM 40.0-010, page 2, or 40_Wheels_and_Wheel_Alignment_volume_1.pdf, page 4). \n\nFor supporting the vehicle on a lift or with jack stands, place mounting blocks on the **front axle at the inner frame side members** (1) and at the **rear axle on the supporting plates of the rear axle carrier** (21 front bearing) (refer to Fig. 6 and 7 in FSM 40.0-010, page 2, or 40_Wheels_and_Wheel_Alignment_volume_1.pdf, page 4). \n\nAlternatively, 'jacking-up shoes' can be used with supporting jacks, plugging into the frame floor (refer to Fig. 8 and 9 in FSM 40.0-010, page 3, or 40_Wheels_and_Wheel_Alignment_volume_1.pdf, page 5).\n\nAlways secure the vehicle against moving off by placing chocks against the wheels before applying the jack, and use appropriate jack stands once the vehicle is lifted (FSM 00.0-1 AB Service Manual, page 12).",
    "diagnostic_reasoning": "The reported symptom is a collapsed jack point due to rust, indicating a structural integrity failure at that specific location. The immediate need is to safely lift the vehicle using alternative, structurally sound jacking and support points. The FSM provides explicit instructions and diagrams for various lifting methods and designated support locations on the W107 chassis, including the front axle carrier, inner frame side members, and rear axle carrier.",
